  <raw>Shoppers ignore hybrids in China



BEIJING --
You'll be able to buy a General Motors hybrid in China in 2008, says Kevin Wale, president of the General Motors China Group.
The question is: Will anyone want to buy a hybrid? Usually, an automaker assembles models it believes the market wants. But in China, where government relations are an important business tool, automakers are hoping hybrids will do the trick.

China wants to clean up its air and reduce oil consumption. So the powerful central government included a clause encouraging hybrid technology in its July 2004 automotive policy.
Hybrids use internal combustion engines and one or more electric motors to drive the wheels.
Toyota Motor Corp. was the first foreign automaker to assemble a hybrid - the Prius - in China. Sales are slow, but Toyota never expected it to be a big seller, said Yoshimi Inaba, executive vice president of Toyota Motor. Inaba and Wale spoke at the Automotive News China Conference here last week.
Local automaker SAIC Motor Corp. is working on its own hybrid model because the Shanghai government asked it to, says a former SAIC manager.
Chery Automobile Co. also plans to offer a hybrid model.
The most important thing Chinese consumers seek when car shopping is value for money, which includes gadgets and a roomy interior. Few shoppers will spend money to help the environment.
Market forecaster CSM Worldwide reckons the market for hybrids in China by 2012 will be a scant 10,000 units.
China's cities have some of the world's dirtiest air. So fuel-efficient hybrids will help clean the air. If the government passes policies such as tax breaks to encourage hybrid sales, demand could increase.
But, so far, hybrids in China are being driven by government policy, not consumers.</raw>
